And what al-Tirmidhi narrated from Abdullah ibn Umar: The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Indeed Allah placed truth on the tongue of Umar and his heart.' Ibn Umar said: No matter ever befell the people and they spoke about it, and Umar spoke, but the Quran was revealed in accordance with what Umar said. And what is in the two Sahihs from Masruq and Shaqiq: They said: Abdullah said: By Him besides Whom there is no god, no sura was revealed in the Book of Allah except that I know where it was revealed, and no verse was revealed in the Book of Allah except that I know in what matter it was revealed. If I knew anyone more knowledgeable than myself of the Book of Allah whom the camels could reach, I would ride to him. And what is in al-Tirmidhi from Abu Musa, may Allah be pleased with him, he said: 'Nothing was unclear to us, the Companions...' And the reasoning of Abu Sa'id, may Allah have mercy on him: that acting upon their opinion is preferable for two reasons: one is the possibility of hearing and direct instruction, that being a fundamental quality in them, advanced over personal opinion, and they would be silent regarding chains of transmission. And for the possibility of the superiority of their correctness in personal opinion itself; thus this way is the utmost in acting upon the sunna, so that the sunna in all its aspects and similarities is advanced over analogy. Then analogy in its strongest forms is a proof, and it is the correct meaning confirmed by its established effect according to the Law. And al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, squandered most aspects of the sunan, then inclined to analogy of resemblance, which is not suitable for attributing obligation to it, so he is nothing like one who abandons analogy and acts upon istishab al-hal, thus making caution a ladder to acting without evidence. So the ultimate path in the principles of Sharia and its branches in perfection is the path of our companions, praise be to Allah; to them the religion ended in its perfection, and by their fatwas the Sharia stood until the end of time with its characteristics, but it is a deep sea which not every swimmer can cross, and conditions are many which not every seeker can meet. And this difference [is] in everything established from them without disagreement among them, and without it being established that that statement reached someone other than its speaker, and he remained silent in submission to it. The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, a hadith, 'We asked Aisha about it: we only found knowledge of it with her,' and Allah is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 530

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Hanafi methodology, specifically the preference given to the practice and opinions of the Companions over qiyas (analogy). The author begins by citing numerous hadiths that attest to the profound knowledge and virtue of the Companions, especially Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali, and others. These hadiths establish that the Companions were the most knowledgeable of the community and that their judgments were divinely guided. Building on this, the author presents the reasoning of Abu Sa'id (a Hanafi scholar) that the Companions' opinions are to be given precedence for two reasons: first, their opinions may be based on direct hearing from the Prophet (tawqif), which is a weightier source than personal reasoning; second, even when they used their own judgment, their reasoning was more likely to be correct due to their closeness to revelation. Therefore, their practice is considered the ultimate way to implement the sunna, and qiyas is only to be used after that. The author then criticizes al-Shafi'i for abandoning many aspects of the sunna (embodied in the Companions' practice) and resorting to 'qiyas al-shabah' (analogy of resemblance), which is deemed insufficient to establish obligation. This, the author argues, is akin to using istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity) without evidence. The passage concludes with high praise for the Hanafi school, stating that their path is the perfect one for understanding both principles and branches of Sharia, and that the religion reached its perfection through them. It also notes that those who disagree with a Companions' ruling and remain silent are considered to have submitted to it. This reflects the Hanafi principle that the silence of other Companions indicates consensus.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning to derive a legal ruling for a new case based on an original case due to a common effective cause (illa).
قياس الشبه (qiyas al-shabah): Analogy based on resemblance to multiple origins, not a single clear illa.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity of an existing state until evidence of change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) They may have heard a Prophetic teaching (tawqif) or had superior personal judgment.
C) Qiyas is completely invalid in the Hanafi school.
D) The Companions never disagreed with each other.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prioritizes Companions' practice over qiyas due to possible tawqif and superior judgment; qiyas is only a proof in its strongest form (qiyas al-illa).

The Five Essential Components of Qiyas
Chapter opening outlining the five aspects of qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five essential components of qiyas as outlined by Al-Bazdawi and explain why each is necessary for a valid analogical argument.

Core Idea
Qiyas has five pillars: meaning, condition, pillar, ruling, and refutation—all must be fulfilled.

This opening passage of the chapter on qiyas (analogical reasoning) from Al-Bazdawi's Usul presents a structural roadmap for understanding this critical source of Islamic law. Rather than diving straight into details, the author first delineates five indispensable aspects: the definition, condition, pillar, ruling, and refutation of qiyas. Mastering this framework ensures that your reasoning is grounded, valid, and purposeful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Recognize that the chapter is about qiyas, analogical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence.
2
List the five divisions
Extract each part: tafsir (definition), shart (condition), rukn (pillar), hukm (ruling), and daf' (refutation).
Memorize them in order.
3
Understand the logical sequence
Grasp why each follows from the previous: meaning first, then condition for existence, then pillar for standing, then purpose (ruling), then refuting errors.
This sequence reflects the structure of any valid qiyas argument.
4
Apply to a simple qiyas example
Consider the qiyas of date wine (nabidh) to grape wine. The definition is 'analogy based on shared illa', the condition is that the illa must be clear (e.g., intoxication), the pillar is the shared illa, the ruling is prohibition, and the refutation would address objections like 'date wine has a different taste'.
This illustrates how each division functions in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس الكلام في هذا الباب ينقسم إلى أقسام : أولها: الكلام في تفسير القياس. والثاني : في شرطه . والثالث : في ركنه. والرابع: في حكمه. والخامس: في دَفْعه. ولا بد من معرفة هذه الجملة ؛ لأن الكلامَ لا يصح إلا بمعناه، ولا يوجد إلا عند شرطه ولا يقوم إلا بركنه ولم يُشرع إلا لحكمه، ثم لا يبقى إلا الدفع.
Chapter on Analogy (Qiyas). The discourse in this chapter is divided into sections: First: The discourse on the definition of qiyas. Second: On its condition. Third: On its pillar. Fourth: On its ruling. Fifth: On its refutation. And it is necessary to know this entirety; because the discourse is not valid except through its meaning, nor does it exist except through its condition, nor does it stand except through its pillar, nor is it legislated except for its ruling, then nothing remains except the refutation.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on qiyas (analogical reasoning) by outlining its five essential components. The author, Al-Bazdawi, explains that a proper understanding of qiyas requires studying: (1) its definition (tafsir), (2) its conditions (shart), (3) its pillars (rukn), (4) its ruling (hukm) — i.e., the legal consequences of applying qiyas, and (5) its refutation (daf') — i.e., responding to objections against qiyas. The author then underscores the necessity of mastering these five parts because any discourse on qiyas must be grounded in its proper meaning, must meet its conditions, must be supported by its pillars, and must serve its intended purpose. Only after addressing these can one proceed to refute invalid arguments.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical deduction — deriving a ruling for a new case based on an original case due to a shared effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

The Five Divisions of Qiyas
Lists the five divisions of the chapter on qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the five essential components of qiyas as outlined by al-Bazdawi: tafsir, shart, rukn, hukm, and daf'.

Core Idea
Qiyas has five parts; without all, the discussion is incomplete.

Al-Bazdawi opens the chapter on qiyas by dividing the discussion into five sections. This structural map is crucial for understanding the comprehensive nature of qiyas in Hanafi usul. Each division—interpretation, condition, pillar, ruling, and rebuttal—plays a distinct role, and no qiyas is valid without considering all five.

Guided Path
1
List the five divisions
From the text, extract the five parts: tafsir (interpretation), shart (condition), rukn (pillar), hukm (ruling), and daf‘ (rebuttal).
These are the keywords from the first paragraph.
2
Explain the logic of the sequence
The author states that discussion is not valid without its meaning (tafsir), does not exist without its condition, does not stand without its pillar, is not legislated except for its ruling, and then only rebuttal remains. Trace this logical dependency.
Each division builds on the previous.
3
Understand the necessity of each division
Reflect on why each division is necessary: tafsir gives the concept, shart sets prerequisites, rukn provides the essential structure, hukm defines the purpose, and daf‘ allows for objections.
This shows that qiyas is a holistic legal tool.

الكلام في هذا الباب ينقسم إلى أقسام :
أولها: الكلام في تفسير القياس.
والثاني : في شرطه .
والثالث : في ركنه.
والرابع: في حكمه.
والخامس: في دَفْعه.
ولا بد من معرفة هذه الجملة ; لأن الكلامَ لا يصح إلا بمعناه، ولا يوجد إلا عند شرطه ولا يقوم إلا بركنه ولم يُشرع إلا لحكمه، ثم لا يبقى إلا الدفع.

باب تفسير القياس
للقياس تفسير : هو المراد بظاهر صيغته، ومعنى: هو المراد بدلالة صيغته.
ومثاله: الضرب هو اسم لفعل يُعرف بظاهره، ولمعنى يُعقل بدلالته، على ما قلنا.
أما الثابت بظاهر صيغته فالتقدير، يقال: قس النَّعْل بالنعل أي: احده به وقدره به وذلك أن يُلحق الشيء بغيره، فيُجعل مثله ونظيره.
وقد يسمى ما يجري بين اثنين من المناظرة: قياساً، وهو مأخوذ من: قايسته قياساً .
وقد يسمى هذا القياس نظراً مجازاً; لأنه من طريق النظر يُدرك.
وقد يسمى : اجتهاداً; لأن ذلك طريقه، فسمي به مجازاً.
وأما المعنى الثابت بدلالة صيغته فهو أنه مَدرَكٌ في أحكام الشرع، مفاصله. ومَفصل من
وهذه جملةٌ لا تُعقَل إلا بالبسط والبيان، وبيان ذلك :
أن الله تعالى كلَّفنا العمل بالقياس بطريق وضعه على مثال العمل بالبينات، فجعل الأصول شهوداً، فهي شهود الله تعالى، ومعنى النصوص : هو شهادتها، وهو العلة الجامعة بين الفرع والأصل.
ولا بدَّ من صلاحية الأصول، وهو كونها صالحةً للتعليل، كصلاحية
Chapter on Qiyas
The discussion in this chapter is divided into sections:
First: discussion on the interpretation of qiyas.
Second: on its condition.
Third: on its pillar.
Fourth: on its ruling.
Fifth: on its rebuttal.
It is necessary to know these matters; because discussion is not valid except with its meaning, it does not exist except with its condition, it does not stand except with its pillar, it is not legislated except for its ruling, and then nothing remains but the rebuttal.

Chapter on the Interpretation of Qiyas
Qiyas has an interpretation: that which is intended by the apparent form of its wording, and a meaning: that which is intended by the implication of its wording.
Its example: الضرب (hitting) is a name for an action known by its apparent meaning, and a meaning understood by its implication, as we said.
As for that which is established by the apparent form of its wording, it is estimation (taqdir). It is said: measure the sandal by the sandal, i.e., match one to the other and estimate it by it, that is, to attach one thing to another and make it its like and equivalent.
Sometimes what occurs between two people in debate is called qiyas, and it is derived from 'I measured him (qayastuhu) in measurement.'
Sometimes this qiyas is metaphorically called nazar (contemplation) because it is perceived by the way of contemplation.
Sometimes it is called ijtihad metaphorically because that is its way.
As for the meaning established by the implication of its wording, it is that it is a means of deriving rulings of the law, its details. And a detailed part of...
This is a summary that cannot be understood except by expansion and explanation, and its explanation is:
That Allah the Exalted has obligated us to act by qiyas in a way He placed on the model of acting with evidence. He made the principles (usul) as witnesses – they are witnesses of Allah. And the meaning of the texts is their testimony, which is the common effective cause (illa) between the branch and the original.
It is necessary that the principles be suitable, i.e., that they are suitable for ta'lil (causal reasoning), like the suitability of...
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the chapter on qiyas in al-Bazdawi's Usul. The author first divides the chapter into five parts: definition (tafsir), conditions (shart), pillar (rukn), ruling (hukm), and rebuttal (daf'). He emphasizes that understanding these five is essential because any discussion of qiyas must have a proper meaning, meet its conditions, have its pillar, and serve its purpose; otherwise only rebuttal remains.

Then he begins the definitional section. He states that qiyas has two meanings: one is the apparent linguistic meaning (ma'na bi-zahir sigha) and the other is the technical meaning (ma'na bi-dalala sigha). The linguistic meaning is taqdir (estimation/comparison), exemplified by saying 'measure the sandal by the sandal' meaning to match one to the other. It is also used for debate (munazara) and sometimes called nazar (contemplation) or ijtihad metaphorically because qiyas is the path to those.

The technical meaning is that qiyas is a means of deriving legal rulings for branch cases (furu') by reference to principles (usul). The author gives a conceptual analogy: Allah has made us act by qiyas on the pattern of acting with legal evidence (bayyinat). The usul are like witnesses, and the texts (nusus) give testimony, which is the common effective cause (illa) connecting the branch (far') and the original (asl). The principles must be suitable for causal reasoning (ta'lil). The passage ends incomplete but clearly aims to establish qiyas as a valid source of law by analogy with testimony.

Note: This text is from the Hanafi school of usul. The author al-Bazdawi is a major Hanafi usuli, and this work is foundational.

Key Terms
التفسير (al-tafsir): Interpretation; here it refers to the first division: discussing the meaning of qiyas.
الشرط (al-shart): Condition; the prerequisites for applying qiyas.
الركن (al-rukn): Pillar; the essential structure of the qiyas argument.
الحكم (al-hukm): Ruling; the legal outcome or purpose of the qiyas.
الدفع (al-daf‘): Rebuttal; addressing objections to the qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many sections does the author divide the discussion of qiyas into?
A) Three: interpretation, condition, and ruling.
B) Five: interpretation, condition, pillar, ruling, and rebuttal.
C) Four: principle, branch, cause, and ruling.
D) Two: linguistic and technical.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas in Hanafi usul comprises five interconnected divisions: interpretation, condition, pillar, ruling, and rebuttal.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
12 min
The Definition of Qiyas: Linguistic and Technical
Defines qiyas linguistically and technically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the linguistic and technical meanings of qiyas and explain the analogy of witnesses (shuhud) used by al-Bazdawi.

Core Idea
Qiyas means both estimation (taqdir) and a legal method where usul act as witnesses.

Al-Bazdawi now delves into the definition of qiyas. He distinguishes its apparent linguistic meaning—estimation (taqdir)—from its implied technical meaning—a method of deriving legal rulings. To illustrate the technical meaning, he provides a powerful analogy: the principles (usul) are like witnesses in court, and the common effective cause (illa) is like their testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of qiyas
The author states that qiyas has an interpretation (tafsir) in its apparent form (zahir) and a meaning (ma‘na) in its implication (dalala). The linguistic meaning is taqdir (estimation). The technical meaning is that it is a means of deriving legal rulings.
The word 'tafsir' here means the twofold definition.
2
Explicate the linguistic meaning: taqdir
The author gives the example 'measure the sandal by the sandal'—matching one thing to another. This is the literal sense of qiyas as comparison or estimation.
He also notes that qiyas is used for debate (munazara) and metaphorically called nazar or ijtihad.
3
Unpack the technical meaning and the witness analogy
The technical meaning is that qiyas is a means (madrak) for deriving rulings of the law in detail. Allah has patterned it after the use of evidence (bayyinat): the usul are like witnesses (shuhud), the meaning of the texts is their testimony (shahada), which is the common illa between the branch (far‘) and the original (asl).
This analogy grounds qiyas in a juridical framework.
4
Note the requirement for the usul
The usul must be suitable (saliha) for causal reasoning (ta‘lil). Not every ruling can be used as an asl; it must have a discernible illa.
This is a condition for valid qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس
الكلام في هذا الباب ينقسم إلى أقسام :
أولها: الكلام في تفسير القياس.
والثاني : في شرطه .
والثالث : في ركنه.
والرابع: في حكمه.
والخامس: في دَفْعه.
ولا بد من معرفة هذه الجملة ; لأن الكلامَ لا يصح إلا بمعناه، ولا يوجد إلا عند شرطه ولا يقوم إلا بركنه ولم يُشرع إلا لحكمه، ثم لا يبقى إلا الدفع.

باب تفسير القياس
للقياس تفسير : هو المراد بظاهر صيغته، ومعنى: هو المراد بدلالة صيغته.
ومثاله: الضرب هو اسم لفعل يُعرف بظاهره، ولمعنى يُعقل بدلالته، على ما قلنا.
أما الثابت بظاهر صيغته فالتقدير، يقال: قس النَّعْل بالنعل أي: احده به وقدره به وذلك أن يُلحق الشيء بغيره، فيُجعل مثله ونظيره.
وقد يسمى ما يجري بين اثنين من المناظرة: قياساً، وهو مأخوذ من: قايسته قياساً .
وقد يسمى هذا القياس نظراً مجازاً; لأنه من طريق النظر يُدرك.
وقد يسمى : اجتهاداً; لأن ذلك طريقه، فسمي به مجازاً.
وأما المعنى الثابت بدلالة صيغته فهو أنه مَدرَكٌ في أحكام الشرع، مفاصله. ومَفصل من
وهذه جملةٌ لا تُعقَل إلا بالبسط والبيان، وبيان ذلك :
أن الله تعالى كلَّفنا العمل بالقياس بطريق وضعه على مثال العمل بالبينات، فجعل الأصول شهوداً، فهي شهود الله تعالى، ومعنى النصوص : هو شهادتها، وهو العلة الجامعة بين الفرع والأصل.
ولا بدَّ من صلاحية الأصول، وهو كونها صالحةً للتعليل، كصلاحية
Chapter on Qiyas
The discussion in this chapter is divided into sections:
First: discussion on the interpretation of qiyas.
Second: on its condition.
Third: on its pillar.
Fourth: on its ruling.
Fifth: on its rebuttal.
It is necessary to know these matters; because discussion is not valid except with its meaning, it does not exist except with its condition, it does not stand except with its pillar, it is not legislated except for its ruling, and then nothing remains but the rebuttal.

Chapter on the Interpretation of Qiyas
Qiyas has an interpretation: that which is intended by the apparent form of its wording, and a meaning: that which is intended by the implication of its wording.
Its example: الضرب (hitting) is a name for an action known by its apparent meaning, and a meaning understood by its implication, as we said.
As for that which is established by the apparent form of its wording, it is estimation (taqdir). It is said: measure the sandal by the sandal, i.e., match one to the other and estimate it by it, that is, to attach one thing to another and make it its like and equivalent.
Sometimes what occurs between two people in debate is called qiyas, and it is derived from 'I measured him (qayastuhu) in measurement.'
Sometimes this qiyas is metaphorically called nazar (contemplation) because it is perceived by the way of contemplation.
Sometimes it is called ijtihad metaphorically because that is its way.
As for the meaning established by the implication of its wording, it is that it is a means of deriving rulings of the law, its details. And a detailed part of...
This is a summary that cannot be understood except by expansion and explanation, and its explanation is:
That Allah the Exalted has obligated us to act by qiyas in a way He placed on the model of acting with evidence. He made the principles (usul) as witnesses – they are witnesses of Allah. And the meaning of the texts is their testimony, which is the common effective cause (illa) between the branch and the original.
It is necessary that the principles be suitable, i.e., that they are suitable for ta'lil (causal reasoning), like the suitability of...
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage is an introduction to the chapter on qiyas in al-Bazdawi's Usul. The author first divides the chapter into five parts: definition (tafsir), conditions (shart), pillar (rukn), ruling (hukm), and rebuttal (daf'). He emphasizes that understanding these five is essential because any discussion of qiyas must have a proper meaning, meet its conditions, have its pillar, and serve its purpose; otherwise only rebuttal remains.

Then he begins the definitional section. He states that qiyas has two meanings: one is the apparent linguistic meaning (ma'na bi-zahir sigha) and the other is the technical meaning (ma'na bi-dalala sigha). The linguistic meaning is taqdir (estimation/comparison), exemplified by saying 'measure the sandal by the sandal' meaning to match one to the other. It is also used for debate (munazara) and sometimes called nazar (contemplation) or ijtihad metaphorically because qiyas is the path to those.

The technical meaning is that qiyas is a means of deriving legal rulings for branch cases (furu') by reference to principles (usul). The author gives a conceptual analogy: Allah has made us act by qiyas on the pattern of acting with legal evidence (bayyinat). The usul are like witnesses, and the texts (nusus) give testimony, which is the common effective cause (illa) connecting the branch (far') and the original (asl). The principles must be suitable for causal reasoning (ta'lil). The passage ends incomplete but clearly aims to establish qiyas as a valid source of law by analogy with testimony.

Note: This text is from the Hanafi school of usul. The author al-Bazdawi is a major Hanafi usuli, and this work is foundational.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Linguistically: estimation/comparison. Technically: a means of deriving legal rulings by connecting a branch case to a principle via a common effective cause (illa).
التقدير (al-taqdir): Estimation or making equal; the linguistic sense of qiyas as measuring one thing by another.
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony; in the analogy, the meaning of the texts (the illa) is the testimony of the usul.
الأصول (al-usul): Principles; the original rulings that serve as the basis for qiyas, likened to witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In al-Bazdawi's witness analogy, what does the 'meaning of the texts' (ma‘na al-nusus) correspond to?
A) The witnesses themselves.
B) The testimony (shahada) of the witnesses.
C) The judge's verdict.
D) The branch case (far‘).

Key Takeaway
Qiyas has a dual definition: linguistic (taqdir) and technical (a legal method where usul testify through the illa).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
beginner
6 min
The Definition and Authority of Qiyas (Analogy) in Hanafi Usul
Defining qiyas and proving its validity via analogy and consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the literal and technical meanings of qiyas, describe its validation through the testimony analogy and consensus, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Zahiri rejection.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a valid source extending rulings from texts, proven by analogy with testimony and agreement of the Companions.

This lesson unpacks al-Bazdawi's definition of qiyas and its proof. You'll see how qiyas is not arbitrary but follows a structured process modeled on legal testimony. Understanding this foundation is essential before applying qiyas to derive rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of qiyas
The passage distinguishes between the apparent meaning (linguistic) and the implication (technical) of the term qiyas. Note the example: 'measure the sandal by the sandal' for its literal sense, and 'a means of deriving Shar'i rulings' for its technical sense.
The technical meaning is what matters for usul.
2
Map the testimony analogy
Al-Bazdawi likens qiyas to a courtroom: the original sources (usul) are witnesses, their texts are testimony, the 'illah (cause) is the content of the testimony, the jurist is the plaintiff, the heart is the judge, and the body/opponent is the defendant. Each element has conditions (e.g., source must be suitable for ta'lil).
This analogy demonstrates that qiyas is a valid method because it mirrors the process of evidence.
3
Recognize the role of consensus
The author asserts that the Companions, Followers, and righteous scholars agreed on the validity of qiyas for extending rulings. This consensus (ijma') is a proof for the Hanafi position.
The passage says qiyas is a 'means' (madrak), not a proof for establishing initial rulings.
4
Contrast with the Zahiri position
Dawud al-Zahiri and his followers rejected qiyas entirely. The Hanafi response is based on the testimony analogy and the reported consensus of the early generations.
This ikhtilaf affects whether new cases can be ruled by analogy or only by explicit text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب تفسير القياس
للقياس تفسير : هو المراد بظاهر صيغته، ومعنى: هو المراد بدلالة صيغته.
ومثاله: الضرب هو اسم لفعل يُعرف بظاهره، ولمعنى يُعقل بدلالته ، : على ما قلنا.
أما الثابت بظاهر صيغته فالتقدير، يقال: قس النَّعْل بالنعل : أي: احده به وقدره به وذلك أن يُلحق الشيء بغيره، فيُجعل مثله ونظيره.
وقد يسمى ما يجري بين اثنين من المناظرة: قياساً، وهو مأخوذ من: قايسته قياساً .
وقد يسمى هذا القياس نظراً: مجازاً؛ لأنه من طريق النظر يُدرك.
وقد يسمى : اجتهاداً؛ لأن ذلك طريقه، فسمي به مجازاً.
وأما المعنى الثابت بدلالة صيغته فهو أنه مَدرَكٌ في أحكام الشرع، مفاصله. ومَفصل من
وهذه جملةٌ لا تُعقَل إلا بالبسط والبيان، وبيان ذلك :
أن الله تعالى كلَّفنا العمل بالقياس بطريق وضعه على مثال العمل بالبينات، فجعل الأصول شهوداً، فهي شهود الله تعالى، ومعنى النصوص : هو شهادتها، وهو العلة الجامعة بين الفرع والأصل.
ولا بدَّ من صلاحية الأصول، وهو كونها صالحةً للتعليل، كصلاحية الشهود بالحرية والعقل، والبلوغ.
ولا بد من صلاح الشهادة كصلاح شهادة الشاهد بلفظة : الشهادة: خاصة، وعدالته واستقامته للحكم المطلوب، فكذلك هذه الشهادة.
ولا بد من طالب للحكم على مثال المدعي، وهو القائس.
ولا بد من مطلوب، وهو الحكم الشرعي. ولا بدَّ من مقضي عليه، وهو القلبُ بالعقد ضرورةً، والبَدَنُ بالعمل أصلاً، أو الخصم في مجلس النظر والمحاجة.
ولا بد من حكم هو بمعنى القاضي، وهو القلب.
وإذا ثبت ذلك : بقي للمشهود عليه ولاية الدفع، كما في سائر الشهادات.
هذا مذهب عامة أصحاب النبي (۱) عليه الصلاة والسلام، وهو مذهب عامة التابعين والصالحين، وعلماء الدين رضي الله عنهم أجمعين فإنهم اتفقوا على أن القياس بالرأي على الأصول الشرعية لتعدية أحكامها إلى ما لا نص فيه : مَدْرَكُ من مدارك أحكام الشرع، لا حجة لإثباتها ابتداء.
وقال أصحاب الظواهر من أهل الحديث وغيرهم: إن القياس ليس بحجة، والعمل به باطل، وهو قول داود بن علي الأصبهاني، وغيره.
(۱) باب القياس : قوله : وهذا مذهب عامة الصحابة... إلى آخره: تشهد به كُتُبُ الآثار التي قدمنا ذكرها.
Chapter on the Explanation of Qiyas. Qiyas has an interpretation: what is intended by the apparent wording of its form, and a meaning: what is intended by the implication of its form. An example: 'ضرب' is a name for an act known by its apparent meaning, and for a meaning understood by its implication, as we said. As for what is established by the apparent wording of its form, it is estimation: it is said 'measure the sandal by the sandal', meaning: compare it with it and estimate it by it, and that is to attach one thing to another, making it its like and similar. And it may be called what occurs between two people in debate: qiyas, derived from 'I compared him in qiyas'. And this qiyas may be called 'نظر' (reflection) metaphorically, because it is perceived through the way of reflection. And it may be called 'اجتهاد' (independent reasoning) because that is its path, so it is named by it metaphorically. As for the meaning established by the implication of its form, it is that it is a means of deriving rulings in the laws of Shari'a, its details, and a detailed part of it. This statement cannot be understood except by expansion and explanation, and the explanation of that is: Allah the Exalted has obligated us to act by qiyas in a way He placed it on the model of acting by evidence. He made the sources (usul) as witnesses, so they are the witnesses of Allah the Exalted, and the meaning of the texts is their testimony, and it is the cause ('illah) that unites the branch and the source. It is necessary for the sources to be suitable, that is, they are fit for being used for 'illah, like the suitability of witnesses by freedom, intellect, and puberty. And it is necessary for the testimony to be sound, like the soundness of a witness's testimony with the word of testimony specifically, and his integrity and uprightness for the sought-after judgment; so likewise this testimony. And there must be a seeker of the ruling like the plaintiff, and he is the one performing qiyas. And there must be a sought-after thing, and it is the legal ruling. And there must be a person against whom it is decided, and he is the heart necessarily for belief, and the body for action originally, or the opponent in the council of reflection and argumentation. And there must be a judge who is like the meaning of the judge, and he is the heart. And when that is established, there remains for the one against whom testimony is given the authority to rebut, just as in other testimonies. This is the madhhab of the generality of the Companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and it is the madhhab of the generality of the Followers and the righteous, and the scholars of the religion, may Allah be pleased with them all. For they agreed that qiyas by opinion on the legal sources to extend their rulings to what has no text is a means (madrak) of the means of deriving rulings of the Shari'a, not a proof for establishing them initially. And the people of literal meanings (Zahiris) from the people of hadith and others said: Qiyas is not a proof, and acting by it is invalid, and it is the statement of Dawud bin Ali al-Asbahani and others. (1) Chapter of Qiyas: his statement: 'And this is the madhhab of the generality of the Companions... to its end: the books of narrations that we mentioned earlier testify to it.'
Source pages p. 554

Commentary
This is a matn passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh on the definition and authority of qiyas (analogy). The author first defines qiyas in two ways: (1) by its apparent linguistic meaning, which is estimation or comparison, as in 'measure the sandal by the sandal'; and (2) by its technical meaning, which is a means of deriving Shar'i rulings. He then explains the technical meaning in detail through an elaborate analogy with legal testimony. In this analogy, the original sources (usul) are likened to witnesses, their texts are their testimony, and the 'illah (effective cause) is the meaning that unites the branch and the source. The jurist performing qiyas is the seeker of the ruling like a plaintiff, the heart is the judge, and the body or opponent is the defendant. Just as a witness must be reliable and the testimony sound, the source must be suitable for ta'lil (causal reasoning) and the 'illah must be valid. This analogy demonstrates that qiyas is a valid method because it mirrors the process of evidence in court. The author then asserts that this was the practice of the Companions, Followers, and righteous scholars, who agreed that qiyas is a valid source (madrak) for extending rulings, though not for establishing initial rulings. He refutes the Zahiri position of Dawud al-Zahiri, who denied qiyas entirely. The footnote at the end likely references hadith evidence supporting the claim about the Companions.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Linguistically: estimation or comparison; technically: a means of deriving Shar'i rulings by extending a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a shared 'illah.
العلة (al-'illah): The effective cause or ratio legis that unites the original and branch cases in qiyas; here analogized to the testimony of a witness.
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony; in the analogy, the 'illah is the testimony of the source texts, requiring soundness and suitability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi's analogy, what does the 'illah (cause) represent in relation to qiyas?
A) The witness (shahid) who provides proof
B) The judge (hakim) who decides the ruling
C) The testimony (shahada) that conveys the truth
D) The defendant (mushahad 'alayh) who can rebut

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a valid, structured method for extending Shar'i rulings from texts to new cases, proven by analogy with legal testimony and the consensus of the early generations.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
Qiyas as a Source: Hanafi Position and Zahiri Objections
Analysis of qiyas validity with Zahiri objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on qiyas as a source of law, explain the Zahiri objections, and evaluate the strength of the Zahiri Quranic and hadith proofs.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a valid source to extend rulings, but Zahiris reject it claiming the Quran is sufficient.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh presents the Hanafi position that qiyas (analogical reasoning) is a legitimate source for deriving rulings in new cases, supported by consensus of the Companions and early scholars. It then introduces the Zahiri challenge, which denies qiyas altogether and argues from Quranic verses and a hadith. We will examine the conditions of valid qiyas, the consensus argument, and the Zahiri textual proofs, understanding why this remains a foundational dispute in usul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the testimony analogy
The author compares qiyas to judicial testimony (shahada). Identify the components: the seeker of judgment (qa'is), the ruling sought (hukm), the subject of judgment (heart by contract, body by action), and the judge (heart). This analogy emphasizes the need for rigor and integrity in qiyas.
The analogy implies that qiyas must meet conditions similar to testimony: the qa'is must be upright and qualified.
2
Identify the Hanafi position and its evidential basis
The Hanafis assert that qiyas is a source (madrak) for extending rulings, not for establishing them initially. They support this by citing consensus (ijma) of the Companions and Followers.
The text says 'they agreed' – this is a claim of ijma, a strong proof in Hanafi usul.
3
Classify the Zahiri objections
The Zahiris (led by Dawud al-Isfahani) reject qiyas. The text notes three internal positions: some reject rational proof entirely, some limit it to rational matters, and some accept it but say istishab (presumption of continuity) suffices.
These subdivisions show that even among Zahiris there is methodological diversity.
4
Examine the Zahiri textual proofs
The Zahiris cite Quran 16:89 and 6:59 (the Quran as clarification of all things) to argue no need for qiyas, and a hadith condemning analogy by the Children of Israel. Analyze how these proofs aim to close the door to analogical reasoning.
The hadith is key: does it condemn all qiyas or only corrupt analogy?
5
Consider the Hanafi response
Though the text cuts off, typical Hanafi responses include: the Quran's comprehensiveness is in principles not particulars, qiyas is necessary for deriving rulings; the hadith refers to analogy based on desire, not valid qiyas; and the Companions themselves practiced qiyas.
The Hanafi response relies on practice of the Salaf, which they consider as evidence of consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشهود بالحرية والعقل، والبلوغ.
ولا بد من صلاح الشهادة كصلاح شهادة الشاهد بلفظة : الشهادة: خاصة، وعدالته واستقامته للحكم المطلوب، فكذلك هذه الشهادة.
ولا بد من طالب للحكم على مثال المدعي، وهو القائس.
ولا بد من مطلوب، وهو الحكم الشرعي. ولا بدَّ من مقضي عليه، وهو القلبُ بالعقد ضرورةً، والبَدَنُ بالعمل أصلاً، أو الخصم في مجلس النظر والمحاجة.
ولا بد من حكم هو بمعنى القاضي، وهو القلب.
وإذا ثبت ذلك : بقي للمشهود عليه ولاية الدفع، كما في سائر الشهادات.
هذا مذهب عامة أصحاب النبي (۱) عليه الصلاة والسلام، وهو مذهب عامة التابعين والصالحين، وعلماء الدين رضي الله عنهم أجمعين فإنهم اتفقوا على أن القياس بالرأي على الأصول الشرعية لتعدية أحكامها إلى ما لا نص فيه : مَدْرَكُ من مدارك أحكام الشرع، لا حجة لإثباتها ابتداء.
وقال أصحاب الظواهر من أهل الحديث وغيرهم: إن القياس ليس بحجة، والعمل به باطل، وهو قول داود بن علي الأصبهاني، وغيره.
(۱) باب القياس : قوله : وهذا مذهب عامة الصحابة... إلى آخره: تشهد به كُتُبُ الآثار التي قدمنا ذكرها.
واختلف هؤلاء : فقال بعضهم : لا دليل من قبل العقل أصلاً، والقياس قسم منه .
وقال بعضهم : لا عمل لدليل العقل إلا في الأمور العقلية، دون الشرعية.
وقال بعضهم : هو دليل ضروري، ولا ضرورةً بنا إليه ؛ لإمكان العمل باستصحاب الحال.
واحتج من أبطل القياس بالكتاب والسنة، والمعقول:
أما الكتاب فقول الله تعالى: ﴿وَنَزَّلْنَا عَلَيْكَ الْكِتَبَ تِبْيَنَا لِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ النحل / ٨٩.
وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا رَطْبٍ وَلَا يَابِسٍ إِلَّا فِي كِتَبٍ مُّبِينٍ ﴾ الأنعام / ٥٩ ، ومَن جعل القياس حجةً : لم يَجعل الكتاب كافياً.
وأما السنة: فقولُ النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لم يَزَل أمر بني إسرائيل مستقيماً حتى كثُرت فيهم أولاد السبايا فقاسوا ما لم يكن بما قد كان، فضلوا، وأضلُّوا» (۱).
وأما المعقول فلمعنى في الدليل، ولمعنى في المدلول. أما الدليل : فشبهةٌ في الأصل ؛ لأن النص لم ينطق بشيء من
(۱) حديث : لم يزل أمر بني إسرائيل مستقيماً : أخرجه البزار من حديث عبد الله ابن عمرو، وفي سنده قيس بن الربيع فيه ،مقال ورواه الدارمي وأبو عوانة بإسناد صحيح، من قول عروة، لم يرفعه، والله أعلم.
Witnesses (must possess) freedom, intellect, and puberty. It is necessary that the testimony be sound like the soundness of the witness's testimony by the word 'testimony' specifically, and his uprightness and integrity for the required judgment, so likewise this testimony. It is necessary to have a seeker of judgment similar to the plaintiff, and he is the one who performs analogy (qa'is). It is necessary to have a sought object, which is the legal ruling. It is necessary to have a person against whom judgment is passed, which is the heart by contract necessarily, and the body by action originally, or the opponent in the council of consideration and argument. It is necessary to have a judge, which is the heart. When this is established, the one against whom testimony is given retains the authority to repel, as in other testimonies. This is the doctrine of the generality of the Companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and it is the doctrine of the generality of the Followers, the righteous, and the scholars of religion, may Allah be pleased with them all, for they agreed that analogical reasoning (qiyas) based on opinion upon the legal principles to extend their rulings to what has no text is a source of the sources of legal rulings, not a proof for establishing them initially. The proponents of external meanings (Zahiris) from the people of hadith and others said: Qiyas is not a proof, and acting upon it is invalid. This is the statement of Dawud ibn 'Ali al-Asbahani and others. They disagreed among themselves: some said there is no rational proof at all, and qiyas is part of it. Some said rational proof is only used in rational matters, not in legal matters. Some said it is a necessary proof, but we have no need for it because it is possible to act upon istishab al-hal (presumption of continuity). Those who invalidated qiyas argued from the Book, the Sunnah, and rational reasoning. As for the Book, it is the word of Allah: 'And We have sent down to you the Book as clarification for all things' (16:89). And His word: 'And no moist or dry thing but it is in a clear Book' (6:59). Whoever makes qiyas a proof does not consider the Book sufficient. As for the Sunnah, it is the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'The affair of the Children of Israel remained upright until the children of captives multiplied among them, and they analogized what was not like what had been, so they went astray and led others astray.' As for rational reasoning, it concerns a meaning in the proof and a meaning in the proved. As for the proof, it is an ambiguity in the original because the text did not articulate anything regarding...
Source pages p. 555

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul discusses the validity of qiyas (analogical reasoning) as a source of Islamic law. The author begins by drawing a parallel between qiyas and judicial testimony (shahada), specifying the conditions of qiyas: the mustafti (seeker of the ruling) must be a qualified mujtahid (qa'is), the sought ruling must be a legal ruling (hukm shar'i), the object of judgment is the heart or action, and the judge is the heart (conscience). This analogy underscores the careful procedure required in qiyas. The author then asserts that the consensus (ijma) of the Companions, Followers, and righteous scholars is that qiyas is a valid source (madrak) for extending rulings to new cases where no explicit text (nass) exists, though it does not establish rulings initially (i.e., it requires an existing textual basis). This represents the Hanafi position. The passage then presents the opposing view of the Zahiris (ahl al-zawahir), led by Dawud al-Isfahani, who deny qiyas as a proof and deem it invalid. Their internal divisions are noted: some reject rational proof entirely, others limit it to rational matters, and some accept it but consider it unnecessary due to istishab (presumption of continuity). The text then begins to list their arguments: Quranic verses (16:89, 6:59) claiming the Quran explains all things, so qiyas is superfluous; a hadith criticizing analogical reasoning among the Children of Israel; and a rational argument that unfinished. This sets up a refutation by the Hanafi scholars, which is not fully included in this excerpt.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning that extends a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to a shared effective cause (illa).
الشهادة (al-shahada): Testimony; here used analogically to describe the conditions of qiyas, comparing the qa'is to a witness, the hukm to the subject of testimony, etc.
أصحاب الظواهر (ashab al-zawahir): Zahiris, a school that rejects qiyas and limits legal rulings to literal meanings of texts.
استصحاب الحال (istishab al-hal): Presumption of continuity; the principle that a state of affairs persists until proven otherwise. Used by some Zahiris to argue that no new ruling via qiyas is needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the condition of the 'seeker of judgment' (طالب الحكم) in the analogy of qiyas to testimony?
A) He must be a judge (qadi).
B) He must be the plaintiff (mudda'i).
C) He must be the one performing analogy (qa'is).
D) He must be a witness (shahid).

Key Takeaway
The validity of qiyas in Hanafi usul is grounded in consensus of the Salaf, despite Zahiri objections based on textual sufficiency and a hadith on corrupt analogy.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Refutation of Arguments Against Qiyas
Refutes objections to Qiyas from Quran, Sunnah, and reason

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three categories of arguments against qiyas and apply the Hanafi refutation by distinguishing between speculative analogy and disciplined legal reasoning.

Core Idea
Qiyas is valid: objections from Quran, Sunnah, and reason are refuted by necessity and the distinction between rights of Allah and rights of servants.

This lesson delves into the debate on the validity of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Islamic jurisprudence. The passage presents the objections raised by those who reject analogical reasoning, citing Quranic claims that the Book clarifies everything, a hadith condemning analogy among the Children of Israel, and rational arguments that qiyas is speculative. The Hanafi response clarifies that these objections either misinterpret the texts or do not apply to the disciplined qiyas that preserves the meanings of revelation. You will learn to critically assess these proofs and understand why the Hanafi school maintains qiyas as a necessary source of law when texts are silent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the positions of qiyas rejectors
The passage begins by noting three categories of opponents: those who deny all rational evidence, those who limit reason to non-religious matters, and those who say istishab suffices. Identifying these shows that rejection of qiyas takes multiple forms.
These positions are summarized by the author.
2
Examine the opponent's Quranic proof
Opponents cite (16:89) that the Quran is a clarification for all things. The Hanafi response is that the verse means the Quran provides general principles, not every specific ruling. Acting by qiyas does not contradict this.
The Hanafis argue that the verse is about the comprehensiveness of revelation in principles, not a denial of ijtihad.
3
Examine the opponent's Sunnah proof
Opponents cite a hadith that the Children of Israel went astray when they analogized. The Hanafi response is that this refers to corrupt analogy based on caprice, not the disciplined qiyas that relies on textual indications and proper illa.
The hadith is found in al-Bazzar and others.
4
Analyze the rational objections and the Hanafi response
The rational objections claim that qiyas is speculative (shubha) and that Allah's rights cannot be established by speculation, and that Allah is not obeyed by reason. The Hanafi responds by separating rights of Allah (where speculation is not allowed) from rights of servants (where it is allowed). Examples like war, qibla, and valuations are based on sensory or practical considerations, not pure reason, and necessity drives ijtihad.
The distinction between haqq Allah and haqq al-‘abd is critical here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف هؤلاء : فقال بعضهم : لا دليل من قبل العقل أصلاً، والقياس قسم منه .
وقال بعضهم : لا عمل لدليل العقل إلا في الأمور العقلية، دون الشرعية.
وقال بعضهم : هو دليل ضروري، ولا ضرورةً بنا إليه ؛ لإمكان العمل باستصحاب الحال.
واحتج من أبطل القياس بالكتاب والسنة، والمعقول:
أما الكتاب فقول الله تعالى: ﴿وَنَزَّلْنَا عَلَيْكَ الْكِتَابَ تِبْيَانًا لِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ﴾ النحل / ٨٩.
وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا رَطْبٍ وَلَا يَابِسٍ إِلَّا فِي كِتَابٍ مُّبِينٍ ﴾ الأنعام / ٥٩ ، ومَن جعل القياس حجةً : لم يَجعل الكتاب كافياً.
وأما السنة: فقولُ النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام: «لم يَزَل أمر بني إسرائيل مستقيماً حتى كثُرت فيهم أولاد السبايا فقاسوا ما لم يكن بما قد كان، فضلوا، وأضلُّوا».
وأما المعقول فلمعنى في الدليل، ولمعنى في المدلول. أما الدليل : فشبهةٌ في الأصل ؛ لأن النص لم ينطق بشيء من الأوصاف علةً للحكم، والحكم المطلوب حق الله تعالى، فلا يصح إثباته بما هو شبهة في الأصل مع كمال قدرة صاحب الحق.
وأما الذي في المدلول : فلأن المدلول طاعة الله تعالى، ولا يُطاع الله تعالى بالعقول والآراء ، ألا يُرى أن من الشرائع ما لا يُدرك ألبتة بالعقول مثلُ المُقدَّرات، ومنها ما يخالف المعقول.
ولا يلزم أمر الحروب، ودَرْك الكعبة، وتقويم المتلفات
أما على الوجه الأول فلأنها من حقوق العباد.
أما غير القبلة : فلا يُشكل، وأما القبلة : فأصله معرفة أقاليم الأرض، وذلك حق العباد ، فبني على وسعهم.
وأما على الثاني : فلأن هذه الأمور إنما تُعقل بوجوه محسوسة، ألا يُرى أن قيم المتلفات، ومهور النساء وأمور الحرب تُعقل بالأسباب الحسية، وكذلك القبلة، فكان يقيناً بأصله على مثال الكتاب والسنة. وحَصَلَ بما قلنا: المحافظة على النصوص بمعانيها، ولأن العمل بالأصل في مواضع القياس ممكن ، وذلك دليل دعينا إلى العمل به. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ قُل لَا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَى مُحَرَّمًا عَلَى طَاعِمٍ يَطْعَمُهُ﴾ الآية الأنعام ١٤٥ .
وليس كذلك ما ذكرنا من أمور الحرب وغيرها؛ لأن العمل بالأصل غير ممكن.
وكذلك أمر القبلة فعملنا بالاجتهاد للضرورة.
ولا يلزم عليه الاعتبارُ بمَن مضى من القرون في المثلات
And these people differed: some said there is no evidence from reason at all, and Qiyas is part of it. And some said the evidence of reason is not operative except in rational matters, not in religious matters. And some said it is a necessary evidence, and we have no need of it because it is possible to act by istishab al-hal. And those who nullified Qiyas argued by the Book, the Sunnah, and the rational: As for the Book, the saying of Allah the Exalted: 'And We sent down to you the Book as a clarification for all things' (al-Nahl 16:89). And His saying: 'And no moist nor dry but is in a clear Book' (al-An'am 6:59). And whoever made Qiyas a proof did not consider the Book sufficient. And as for the Sunnah, the saying of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The affair of the Children of Israel was upright until the children of captive women increased among them; they analogized what was not from what had been, and they went astray and caused others to go astray.' And as for the rational, for a meaning in the evidence and a meaning in the thing proved. As for the evidence: it is a speculation in the origin, because the text did not specify any attribute as the cause for the ruling, and the sought ruling is a right of Allah the Exalted, so it is not correct to establish it by that which is a speculation in the origin along with the complete power of the right-holder. And as for that which is in the thing proved: because the thing proved is obedience to Allah the Exalted, and Allah is not obeyed by intellects and opinions. Do you not see that among the legislations there are those which are not perceived at all by intellects, like the fixed measures, and among them those which contradict reason. And the matter of wars, finding the direction of the Kaaba, and evaluating damaged goods do not follow as a necessary consequence. As for the first aspect: because they are rights of the servants. As for other than the qibla: it is not problematic. And as for the qibla: its origin is knowledge of the zones of the earth, and that is a right of the servants, so it is built upon their capacity. And as for the second aspect: because these matters are comprehended by sensory means. Do you not see that the values of damaged goods, the dowries of women, and the affairs of war are comprehended by sensory causes, and likewise the qibla, so it is certain in its origin in the manner of the Book and the Sunnah. And it is obtained by what we said: preservation of the texts in their meanings. And because acting by the original in places of Qiyas is possible, and that is an evidence that we are called to act by it. Allah the Exalted said: 'Say: I find not in that which is revealed to me anything forbidden for an eater to eat' (al-An'am 6:145). And the matter is not like what we mentioned of the affairs of war and others, because acting by the original is not possible. And likewise the matter of the qibla: we act by ijtihad due to necessity. And the consideration of what has passed of generations in punishments does not follow as a necessary consequence.
Source pages p. 556

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the arguments of those who reject Qiyas (analogical reasoning) in Islamic law. It begins by summarizing three positions of Qiyas' opponents: some deny any rational proof; others limit reason to non-legal matters; and others consider it unnecessary because istishab (presumption of continuity) suffices. Then it presents their proofs from the Quran (verses claiming that the Quran explains everything), the Sunnah (hadith about Israelites going astray by qiyas), and rational arguments that Qiyas relies on speculative attribution (shubha) and that divine commands cannot be known by reason. The Hanafi response refutes these: the rational objections are answered by distinguishing between rights of Allah and rights of servants, and by noting that some matters (like qibla direction) require ijtihad due to practical necessity. The text emphasizes that Qiyas preserves the meanings of texts and is necessary when acting by the original is impossible. It concludes that the objections do not apply to matters of war, qibla, and valuations because they are based on sensory or practical considerations, not pure reason.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause (illa).
الدليل (al-dalil): Evidence or proof that leads to a legal ruling; here it refers to textual or rational evidence.
العلة (al-illa): The effective cause or ratio legis that justifies the legal ruling and is the basis for qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The hadith about Israelites and analogy; Hanafis say it refers to corrupt analogy, not all analogy.
B) The hadith about 'my ummah will not agree on error'; Hanafis say it supports ijma, not qiyas.
C) The hadith 'follow the majority'; Hanafis say it supports consensus.
D) The hadith about the two judges; Hanafis say it supports qiyas.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi refutation of arguments against qiyas shows that qiyas is a disciplined method of preserving textual meanings, necessary when texts are silent.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Defense of Qiyas: Reason in Divine Law
Defends qiyas against objections that reason has no place in divine law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the validity of qiyas and respond to objections using textual and rational evidence.

Core Idea
Qiyas is valid for rulings based on observable causes; purely devotional matters are exceptions left to revelation.

This passage defends qiyas (analogical reasoning) against those who argue that divine law cannot be established by human reason. The Hanafi response carefully distinguishes between purely devotional rulings ('ala al-ta'abbud) and those grounded in observable causes. We will unpack the opponent's arguments, the Hanafi evidence, and the resulting practical difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's position
Locate the objections against qiyas: rulings are rights of Allah, human reason is doubtful, and some rulings (muqaddarat) are beyond reason.
Opponent likely refers to ahl al-zahir or rationalists.
2
State the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal distinguishes between matters of worship and worldly matters based on sensory causes. Wars, qibla, and valuation are understood by experience.
The response uses examples from warfare and qibla to show that reason is already used.
3
Examine the textual evidence
Analyze the Quranic verse 'fai'tabiru ya uli al-absar' (al-Hashr 59:2) and the hadith of the cat. Understand how they support qiyas.
Al-i'tibar is defined as referring something to its equivalent.
4
Distinguish between categories of rulings
Note that purely devotional matters (e.g., fixed measures) are exceptions; they are known only by revelation. Qiyas applies where a cause can be identified.
This distinction is key to the Hanafi argument.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: qiyas is permissible to derive new rulings not explicitly mentioned in texts, except where textual prohibition or purely devotional nature prevents it.
The dispute determines whether new cases can be analogized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأوصاف علةً للحكم، والحكم المطلوب حق الله تعالى، فلا يصح إثباته بما هو شبهة في الأصل مع كمال قدرة صاحب الحق. وأما الذي في المدلول : فلأن المدلول طاعة الله تعالى، ولا يُطاع الله تعالى بالعقول والآراء ، ألا يُرى أن من الشرائع ما لا يُدرك ألبتة بالعقول مثلُ المُقدَّرات، ومنها ما يخالف المعقول. ولا يلزم أمر الحروب، ودَرْك الكعبة، وتقويم المتلفات أما على الوجه الأول فلأنها من حقوق العباد. أما غير القبلة : فلا يُشكل، وأما القبلة : فأصله معرفة أقاليم الأرض، وذلك حق العباد ، فبني على وسعهم. وأما على الثاني : فلأن هذه الأمور إنما تُعقل بوجوه محسوسة، ألا يُرى أن قيم المتلفات، ومهور النساء وأمور الحرب تُعقل بالأسباب الحسية، وكذلك القبلة، فكان يقيناً بأصله على مثال الكتاب والسنة. وحَصَلَ بما قلنا: المحافظة على النصوص بمعانيها، ولأن العمل بالأصل في مواضع القياس ممكن ، وذلك دليل دعينا إلى العمل به. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ قُل لَا أَجِدُ فِي مَا أُوحِيَ إِلَى مُحَرَّمَا عَلَى طَاعِمٍ يَطْعَمُهُ الله الآية الأنعام ١٤٥ . وليس كذلك ما ذكرنا من أمور الحرب وغيرها؛ لأن العمل بالأصل غير ممكن. وكذلك أمر القبلة فعملنا بالاجتهاد للضرورة. ولا يلزم عليه الاعتبارُ بمَن مضى من القرون في المثلات والكرامات ؛ لأن ذلك أمر يُعقل بالحس والعيان. وعلى ذلك يُحمل ما ورد في الكتاب من الأمر بالاعتبار، وعلى أمر الحرب تُحمل مشاورة النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام. من أدلة المحتجين القياس : وعامة العلماء وأئمة الهدى الكتاب والسنة، والدليل المعقول، وهذا أكثر من أن يُحصى، وأوضح من أن يخفى، وإنما نذكر طرفاً منه ; تبركا، واقتداء بالسلف. قال الله تعالى: ﴿ فَاعْتَبِرُوا يَتَأَولِي الْأَبْصَر الحشر / ۲ ، والاعتبار: ردُّ الشيء إلى نظيره والعبرة البيانُ قال الله تعالى: إن كُنتُم للرة يَا تَعْبُرُونَ )) يوسف / ٤٣ : أي تبينون، والقياس مثله سواء. وهو فإن قيل: عندي إنما يصح الاعتبار بأمرٍ ثابت بالنص دون الرأي، وأن يُذكر سبب هلاك قوم، أو نجاتهم، وكذلك عندي ها هنا إذا ذكرت العله نصاً، مثل قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في الهرة: «إنها من الطوافين عليكم والطوافات).
Attributes are the cause for the ruling, and the required ruling is a right of Allah the Exalted, so it is not valid to establish it by what is a doubt in origin while the possessor of the right has complete power. As for that which pertains to the object of the ruling: because the object of the ruling is obedience to Allah the Exalted, and Allah is not obeyed by intellects and opinions. Do you not see that among the laws there are those that cannot be grasped by intellects at all, such as the fixed measures, and among them those that contradict reason? But the matter of wars, finding the Ka'ba, and valuing lost goods do not necessitate [a problem]. On the first aspect, because they are among the rights of servants. As for other than the qibla: it is not problematic. As for the qibla: its origin is knowledge of the climes of the earth, and that is a right of servants, so it was built on their capacity. On the second aspect: because these matters are only understood by sensory means. Do you not see that the values of lost goods, dowries of women, and affairs of war are understood by sensory causes, and likewise the qibla, so it is certain in its origin on the example of the Book and Sunnah. What we have said results in preserving the texts according to their meanings. And because acting upon the original in places of qiyas is possible, that is evidence calling us to act upon it. Allah the Exalted said: 'Say: I do not find in what has been revealed to me anything forbidden to a eater who eats it' [al-An'am 6:145]. But the matters of war and others we mentioned are not like that; because acting upon the original is not possible. Likewise the matter of the qibla: we act by ijtihad out of necessity. And the consideration of past generations in punishments and miracles is not necessary; because that matter is understood by sense and sight. And upon that is carried what came in the Book of the command to consider, and upon the matter of war is carried the consultation of the Prophet, peace be upon him. Among the proofs of those who argue for qiyas: the generality of scholars and leaders of guidance [quote] the Book, the Sunnah, and rational evidence. This is more than can be counted and clearer than to be hidden. We only mention a portion of it as a blessing and following the predecessors. Allah the Exalted said: 'So take heed, O people of insight' [al-Hashr 59:2]. And al-i'tibar is referring something to its equivalent, and al-ibra is clarification. Allah the Exalted said: 'If you are for the dream you interpret' [Yusuf 12:43], i.e., you clarify. And qiyas is the same. And if it is said: According to me, consideration is only valid by a matter established by text, not by opinion, and that the cause of destruction or salvation of a people be mentioned. And likewise according to me here if the cause is mentioned by text, like the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him, about the cat: 'It is of those who go around among you and go around.'
Source pages p. 557

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi defense of qiyas (analogical reasoning) against opponents who argue that divine law is not subject to human reason. The text first presents the opponent's claim: rulings of Shari'a are rights of Allah, and since human intellect is doubtful, it cannot establish divine obligations. Moreover, some rulings (like fixed measures) are beyond reason, and some even contradict it. The Hanafi response distinguishes between purely devotional matters (ta'abbud) and those based on observable causes. Matters like warfare, determining the qibla, and valuation of lost goods are grounded in sensory experience and common custom, making qiyas appropriate. The text then cites Quranic evidence: 'So take heed, O people of insight' (al-Hashr 59:2), defining i'tibar as referring something to its equivalent. It also cites the story of Yusuf where 'tabirun' means interpreting/clarifying. The author addresses a further objection that i'tibar only applies to matters explicitly mentioned in revelation (like historical punishments). The Hanafi response is that such examples are understood by sense and sight, and the general command to consider applies to all analogous situations. The passage concludes by highlighting the consensus of scholars (jama'at al-ulama') in using qiyas.

Key Terms
الاعتبار (al-iʿtibār): رد الشيء إلى نظيره (referring something to its equivalent).
القياس (al-qiyās): مثل الاعتبار سواء (same as i'tibar).
العبرة (al-ʿibra): البيان (clarification).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'al-iʿtibār'?
A) Clarification (al-bayān)
B) Referring something to its equivalent
C) Deduction by pure reason
D) Following the apparent meaning of the text

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is a valid source for legal rulings when grounded in observable causes, as affirmed by Quranic command and prophetic practice.

Quranic Proof for Qiyas: The Command of I'tibar
Quranic proof for Qiyas via the command of i'tibar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Quranic command 'fa'tabiru' serves as a proof for Qiyas in Hanafi usul and analyze the objection that i'tibar is restricted to textual matters.

Core Idea
The Quran commands i'tibar (consideration), which is identical to Qiyas—referring a matter to its like.

This lesson examines the primary Quranic proof for Qiyas used by Hanafi scholars: the verse {So consider, O people of insight} (59:2). We will unpack the definition of i'tibar, see how it equates to Qiyas, and address an early objection that i'tibar only applies to textual causes, using the example of the cat hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the verse and its command
Locate the verse {So consider, O people of insight} (59:2) in the source text. Note the imperative form 'i'tabiru' (consider). This is a direct command from Allah.
2
Define i'tibar and equate to Qiyas
The author defines i'tibar as 'referring something to its like' (radd al-shay' ila nazirihi). This is the essence of Qiyas. Also note the verse on dream interpretation (12:43) where 'ta'bir' (interpretation) is equated with explanation, further reinforcing the connection.
In Hanafi usul, i'tibar and qiyas are synonymous.
3
Address the objection about textual basis
An objector claims that i'tibar is only valid when based on a textual cause (nas), not on opinion (ra'y). They cite the hadith of the cat, where the Prophet gave a textual cause for its purity. The Hanafi response is deferred, but you should note the objection and prepare to revisit it.
The hadith: 'It is not impure; it is one of those who go around you.'

From the proofs of those who argue for Qiyas: ] And for the general scholars and imams of guidance are the Book, the Sunnah, and rational proof, and this is more than can be counted and clearer than can be hidden, and we only mention a portion of it as a blessing and following the predecessors. Allah Almighty said: {So consider, O people of insight} (al-Hashr 59:2), and al-i'tibar (consideration) is: referring something to its like, and al-ibra is explanation. Allah Almighty said: {If you are for the dream you interpret} (Yusuf 12:43) meaning you explain, and Qiyas is exactly the same. And if it is said: In my view, consideration is only valid by a matter established by text, not by opinion, such as mentioning the cause of destruction of a people or their salvation, and similarly here if the cause is mentioned textually, like the statement of the Prophet, peace be upon him, regarding the cat: 'It is of those who go around you and those who go around.' (Hadith: It is not najis: from Kabsa bint Ka'b ibn Malik...) And the answer is what we will explain, God willing. And Allah Almighty said: {Indeed in that are signs for a people who reflect} (ar-Ra'd 13:3) and {who reason} (ar-Ra'd 13:4) and similar. And He, His mention is exalted, said: {And for you in retribution there is life, O people of understanding} (al-Baqarah 2:179), which is annihilation and killing in appearance, but it is life in terms of meaning by its legislation and execution. As for the first: whoever contemplates the legislation of retribution, it prevents him from directly causing it, so he remains alive, and the intended person for killing is safe from him, so he remains alive, so it becomes life for both, meaning survival for both. And as for its execution: because whoever kills a man becomes at war with the heirs of the killed, and they become likewise against him, so no life is safe for them unless the killer is killed, so the life of the first victim's heirs and clans is safe, so they become alive in meaning, and this is not understood except through reflection. And as for the Sunnah, it is more than can be counted. Among it is what is narrated from the Prophet, peace be upon him, when he sent Mu'adh to Yemen and said to him: 'By what will you judge?' He said: 'By what is in the Book of Allah Almighty.' He said: 'If you do not find in the Book of Allah Almighty?' He said: 'I will judge by what the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, has judged.'
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage presents the proofs for Qiyas (analogical reasoning) as a source of Islamic law, defending it against those who restrict consideration ('i'tibar) to textual matters alone. The author begins by noting that the proofs for Qiyas from the Quran, Sunnah, and reason are too numerous to fully enumerate; only a portion is mentioned. The Quranic verse {So consider, O people of insight} (59:2) is cited, explaining that 'i'tibar means referring something to its like, and Qiyas is identical to this. The verse {If you are for the dream you interpret} (12:43) is also adduced, where 'ta'bir' (interpretation) is equated with Qiyas. An objection is anticipated: some argue that valid 'i'tibar must be based on a textual ruling, not on opinion (ra'y). They cite the hadith of the cat, where the Prophet stated that the cat is not impure because it goes around people, implying a textual cause. The author defers the answer. Further Quranic verses are presented: {Indeed in that are signs for a people who reflect} (13:3) and {who reason} (13:4), and the verse on retribution (2:179) is explained in detail: retribution, though seemingly leading to death, actually preserves life by deterring murder and by executing the killer to protect society. This rational reflection is analogous to Qiyas. Finally, the hadith of Mu'adh is mentioned: when the Prophet appointed Mu'adh as a judge in Yemen, he asked him how he would judge. Mu'adh replied that he would judge by the Quran, then by the Sunnah, and the hadith continues (though cut off here) that he would then exercise his own judgment (ijtihad). This demonstrates the Prophet's approval of using reason and analogy when no explicit text exists.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared cause (illa).
الاعتبار (al-i'tibar): Consideration or reflection, specifically referring a matter to its like to derive a ruling.
العبرة (al-ibra): Explanation or lesson derived from a precedent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of 'i'tibar'?
A) Pure reflection on historical events
B) Referring something to its like
C) Following the consensus of scholars
D) Personal opinion without evidence

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command {So consider} provides a primary scriptural basis for Qiyas, as i'tibar is defined as analogical reasoning.

Sunnah and Rational Proofs for Qiyas: The Hadith of Mu'adh and Reflective Verses
Sunnah and rational proofs for Qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the hadith of Mu'adh and Quranic verses on reflection to justify Qiyas as a source of law in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The Prophet's approval of Mu'adh's ijtihad and the Quran's call to reflection both validate Qiyas.

This lesson continues the proofs for Qiyas by turning to the Sunnah and additional Quranic evidence. The famous hadith of Mu'adh, where the Prophet tacitly approved Mu'adh's intention to use his own judgment when no text exists, shows that analogical reasoning is part of the prophetic methodology. Moreover, Quranic verses that command reflection (tafakkur) and reasoning (ta'aqqul) demonstrate that using reason to understand revelation is itself mandated. The passage also unpacks the profound meaning of the retribution verse (2:179) as an example of rational analogy.

Guided Path
1
Examine the Mu'adh hadith
Find the part of the passage that narrates the Prophet's sending of Mu'adh to Yemen. Note the dialogue: the Prophet asks Mu'adh how he will judge, and Mu'adh replies by the Quran, then by the Sunnah. The known continuation (not fully quoted) includes 'then I will exercise my own judgment' (ajtahidu ra'yi), which the Prophet approved.
The source text cuts off, but the full hadith is famous and used by Hanafis as proof for Qiyas.
2
Connect to Quranic verses on reflection
The author cites verses that call for reflection (tafakkur), reasoning (ta'aqqul), and understanding (with the word 'al-albab' in 2:179). These verses show that using the mind to derive lessons from divine commands is not only allowed but encouraged.
The verse on retribution (2:179) is explained in detail: though retribution involves killing, it ultimately preserves life through deterrence.
3
Understand the Hanafi inference
From the Mu'adh hadith, the Hanafi school infers that when no explicit text exists, the judge must use ijtihad, which includes Qiyas. The reflective verses further support that analogical reasoning is a valid method of understanding the Shari'ah.
This completes the scriptural case for Qiyas.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Proofs for Qiyas: Text, Companion Practice, and Reason
Establishes qiyas via textual, companion, and rational proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three proofs (naqli, companion practice, and aqli) for qiyas and evaluate the Hanafi response to criticism of the Mu'adh hadith.

Core Idea
Qiyas is established through the Prophet's approval, companion consensus, and the Quranic command of i'tibar.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul presents the foundational evidence for qiyas as a source of Islamic law. It addresses the famous hadith of Mu'adh, its critics, and provides supporting companion practice and rational reasoning from the Quran. You will learn how Hanafi usul defends qiyas against those who question its textual basis.

Guided Path
1
Examine the primary proof: the Mu'adh hadith
Read the hadith carefully and note the sequential method: Quran → Sunnah → ijtihad. Identify the criticism (weak chain) and the Hanafi response (multiple corroborating paths).
The hadith explicitly includes ijtihad; Hanafis interpret ijtihad to include qiyas.
2
Analyze the supporting companion practice
Review the narrations from Ibn Mas'ud, 'Umar, and Zayd ibn Thabit. They all prescribe the same sequence: Quran → Sunnah → Ijma → ijtihad. This shows the Companions used qiyas.
The author argues that rejecting companion practice is tantamount to rejecting Islam.
3
Understand the rational (aqli) proof from the Quran
The author links the Quranic command of i'tibar (reflection on past punishments) to qiyas. The example of Surah al-Hashr 59:2 shows that expulsion is a punishment caused by disbelief, and the phrase 'first gathering' indicates repetition when the cause exists.
This is a form of qiyas: identifying the illah (disbelief) and applying the hukm (punishment) to similar cases.
4
Trace the istinbat chain from the Quranic verse
Using the istinbat_chain from the toolbox, see how the verse is analyzed: identifying the cause (talil) and then applying qiyas to extend the punishment.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab dispute on the Mu'adh hadith
Understand why some scholars (al-Bukhari, al-Tirmidhi) consider the hadith weak, and why Hanafis accept it. The practical consequence is acceptance or rejection of qiyas.
This dispute does not affect Hanafi reliance on qiyas due to multiple evidence streams.

عن معاذ بن جبل رضي الله عنه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بعثه إلى اليمن قال له : كيف تقضي إذا عرض لك أمر؟ قال : أقضي بما في كتاب الله قال فإن لم يكن في كتاب الله ؟ قال فبسنة رسول الله ، قال : فإن لم
